1.8 outcome single_choice 
You are an expert research assistant. Your task is to classify information from research paper excerpts according to a predefined coding scheme.
Instructions:
Select exactly one number that best applies, based strictly on the provided document.
Return only the number (e.g., 1, 2, 3) with no explanation or additional text.
If the requested information is not present in the excerpt, answer with Z.
Do not guess or infer.
Target Variable: Outcome variable type
Description: ; 
Answer Options: 1: Binary - Two categories (e.g., suicide attempt: yes / no).
2: Nominal categorical (multi-class) - More than two unordered (= nominal) categories (e.g., attachment style: secure / anxious / avoidant / disorganized).
3: Ordinal categorical - Ordered categories with meaningful ranks (e.g., job position levels: intern / junior staff / senior staff / manager / executive).
4: Continuous - Interval or ratio scale with numeric values and meaningful distances (IQ score).

You are an expert research assistant. Your task is to classify information from research paper excerpts according to a predefined coding scheme.
Instructions:
Select exactly one number that best applies, based strictly on the provided document.

Target Variable: Outcome variable type
Description: ; 
Answer Options: 1: Binary - Two categories (e.g., suicide attempt: yes / no).
2: Nominal categorical (multi-class) - More than two unordered (= nominal) categories (e.g., attachment style: secure / anxious / avoidant / disorganized).
3: Ordinal categorical - Ordered categories with meaningful ranks (e.g., job position levels: intern / junior staff / senior staff / manager / executive).
4: Continuous - Interval or ratio scale with numeric values and meaningful distances (IQ score).
Excerpt:
{context}
Answer:
 
**Task**  
You are a research‑assistant tasked with **classifying the type of outcome variable** described in a short excerpt from a research paper.

**Context**  
The excerpt (between the markers `Excerpt:` and `End of excerpt`) is the only source of information you may use. Do **not** rely on any outside knowledge or make assumptions beyond what is explicitly stated.

**Coding scheme (choose the single best option)**  

| Code | Variable type | Definition (examples) |
|------|---------------|------------------------|
| **1** | **Binary** | Exactly two mutually exclusive categories (e.g., “suicide attempt: yes / no”). |
| **2** | **Nominal categorical** | More than two unordered categories (e.g., “attachment style: secure / anxious / avoidant / disorganized”). |
| **3** | **Ordinal categorical** | Categories with a natural order or rank (e.g., “job level: intern → junior → senior → manager → executive”). |
| **4** | **Continuous** | Measured on an interval or ratio scale with numeric values and meaningful distances (e.g., IQ score, blood pressure). |
| **Z** | **Information not present** | The excerpt provides no indication of the outcome‑variable type. |

**Instructions**  

1. **Read the excerpt carefully.** Identify any statement that explicitly describes the outcome variable (its scale, categories, or measurement).  
2. **Select the single code** that best matches that description, using the definitions above.  
   - If more than one code could apply, choose the **most specific** one (e.g., if the variable is described as “a 0–1 score” that is binary, not continuous).  
3. **If the excerpt does not mention the outcome variable at all, or the description is too vague to assign a code, answer `Z`.**  
4. **Do not infer, guess, or add information** that is not directly stated in the excerpt.  
5. **Output only the chosen code** (1, 2, 3, 4, or Z). **No extra text, explanations, or punctuation** may be included.

**Edge‑case handling**  

- **Multiple mentions:** If the excerpt lists several outcomes, use the one that is *explicitly* identified as the primary outcome (the first one mentioned).  
- **Quantitative numbers without a scale:** If numbers are given but no scale or categorisation is described (e.g., “participants scored an average of 7.4”), treat this as **Continuous (4)** only if the underlying variable is clearly a measurement (e.g., “score”, “level”). Otherwise, answer `Z`.  
- **Implicit binary wording:** Phrases like “presence/absence of X” count as **Binary (1)**.  
- **Ambiguous ordering:** If categories are listed with a possible order but the order is not stated as meaningful, treat them as **Nominal (2)**.  

**Excerpt**  
{context}

**Answer**   (write only the code)
 
Example 1:
Extract from paper: <We recorded whether each participant attempted suicide during the 12‑month follow‑up (yes versus no).>
True Answer: 1
Example 2:
Extract from paper: <The primary outcome was the participants' IQ score, measured on a standard scale with a mean of 100 and a standard deviation of 15.>
True Answer: 4

You are an expert research assistant. Your task is to extract numeric information from research paper excerpts.
Instructions:
Provide the numeric value corresponding to the concept, taken strictly from the provided document.
If the concept is not reported or cannot be determined from the excerpt, answer with Z.
Do not guess, estimate, or infer values.
Do not provide any explanation, units, or extra text—only the number or Z.
Target Variable: Number of cases (i.e., the outcome to be predicted) in the minority group
Description: In the context of classification tasks, the number of cases (observations) refers to the frequency with which a specific class of the outcome occurs within the dataset. The category with the smallest frequency should be coded.; If reported, include cases from all validation sets in the total number of cases.

{context}
Answer:


You are an expert research assistant. Your task is to extract numeric information from research paper excerpts.
Instructions:
Provide the numeric value corresponding to the concept, taken strictly from the provided document.

If the concept is not reported or cannot be determined from the excerpt, answer with Z.
Do not guess, estimate, or infer values.
Do not include any extra commentary beyond the specified format.
Target Variable: Number of cases (i.e., the outcome to be predicted) in the minority group
Description: In the context of classification tasks, the number of cases (observations) refers to the frequency with which a specific class of the outcome occurs within the dataset. The category with the smallest frequency should be coded.; If reported, include cases from all validation sets in the total number of cases.
Excerpt:
{context}
Answer:


**Task:** Extract a single numeric value from the supplied excerpt.  
**Context:** The value must be taken *exactly* as it appears in the text—no calculation, conversion, or interpretation is allowed.

---

### Target Variable  
**“Number of cases (i.e., the outcome to be predicted) in the minority group.”**  
- This is the total count of observations belonging to the class with the **fewest** occurrences in the dataset.  
- If the excerpt reports a count that already includes all validation sets, use that number.  
- If separate counts are given for training, validation, and/or test sets, **add them together** *only* when the excerpt explicitly states that the numbers should be summed to obtain the total minority‑class count.  
- **Do not** infer or estimate a total when the excerpt does not provide a clear, explicit total.

---

### Extraction Rules  

1. **Exact Match** – Return the number exactly as it is written (e.g., “12”, “1 234”, “0.5”).  
2. **Missing or Unclear** – If the excerpt does **not** state the minority‑group case count, or the information is ambiguous (e.g., “few cases”, “approximately”, “around”), answer with the single character **`Z`**.  
3. **No Units or Commentary** – Do **not** include any units, commas, words, or explanatory text. Only the plain numeric string or `Z`.  
4. **No Guessing** – Do not perform any arithmetic unless the excerpt explicitly tells you to sum specific numbers.  
5. **Single Output** – Provide **one** token only (the number or `Z`).  

---

### Optional Reasoning (do not output)  
- If you are uncertain whether a number refers to the minority class, double‑check the surrounding sentences for a clear statement such as “the minority class had X cases” or “the smallest class contained X instances”.  
- If multiple numbers appear, select the one that the text directly links to “minority group”, “smallest class”, or “least frequent outcome”.  

---

**Excerpt:**  
{context}

**Answer:**  (write only the extracted number or `Z`)

True Answer: <63/>

True Answer: <Z/>

Answer Options:
A: Hyperparameter values not mentioned
B: Fixed set of hyperparameter values - Instead of tuning hyperparameters, the authors use predefined fixed values.

Answer:


**Task:**  
Classify the information contained in a research‑paper excerpt about *hyper‑parameter values used for model tuning* according to the coding scheme below.

**Context:**  
You will be given the excerpt in the placeholder `{context}`.

**Coding Scheme (select **all** letters that apply):**  

| Letter | Meaning |
|--------|--------------------------------------------------------------|
| **A** | The paper does **not** mention hyper‑parameter values at all. |
| **B** | The authors use a **fixed** set of hyper‑parameter values (no tuning). |
| **C** | The paper states that **default** hyper‑parameter values were used, but does not list the values. |
| **D** | The authors **explicitly list** the specific hyper‑parameter values or ranges that were considered for tuning. |
| **E** | **Other** – the excerpt clearly indicates a correct answer that is not captured by A‑D (e.g., a different categorisation). |
| **Z** | None of the above categories apply because the excerpt provides **no relevant information** about hyper‑parameter values. |

**Instructions (follow *exactly*):**  

1. **Read the excerpt carefully.** Base your decision **solely** on what is explicitly stated in the text. Do **not** infer, guess, or add information that is not present.  
2. **Select every letter that correctly describes the excerpt.**  
   - If more than one category is true, list the letters in **alphabetical order**, separated by commas (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
   - If a single category applies, output the single letter (e.g., `B`).  
3. **If the excerpt mentions a situation that does not fit A‑D,** include **E** (“Other”).  
4. **If the excerpt contains no mention of hyper‑parameter values or related decisions,** output **only** `Z`.  
5. **Do not provide any explanation, justification, or additional text**—the answer must be **exactly** the list of letters (or `Z`).  
6. **Missing or ambiguous information:**  
   - If the excerpt suggests a modelling decision (e.g., software choice) but does not explicitly state the hyper‑parameter values, treat this as *missing* and apply the appropriate category (A, C, or Z) based on what is actually written.  
   - If the wording is ambiguous and you cannot determine with certainty which category applies, default to the **most conservative** choice (the category that requires the least assumption).  

**Target Variable:** Hyperparameter values chosen for tuning  
**Description:** *(provided for reference only; do not output)*

C: Grid search - All possible combinations of predefined hyperparameter values are systematically evaluated.
D: Model-based optimization - Based on previous results, models are built and iteratively updated to predict performance and prioritize promising hyperparameter configurations.

B: Random search - Hyperparameter values are randomly sampled from a predefined range to explore different configurations.
C: Grid search - All possible combinations of predefined hyperparameter values are systematically evaluated.
D: Model-based optimization - Based on previous results, models are built and iteratively updated to predict performance and prioritize promising hyperparameter configurations.

**Task**  
You are an expert research assistant. Your job is to read a short excerpt from a research paper and assign the appropriate code‑letter(s) that describe the **method used for hyper‑parameter tuning**. Use **only** the information that is explicitly present in the excerpt.

**Context**  
The excerpt will be supplied in the placeholder `{context}`.

**Coding Scheme**  

| Letter | Meaning (exact wording to use) |
|--------|--------------------------------|
| **A** | “Tuning method not specified, stated that default method was used – The authors do not specify the tuning method and state only that the default procedure of the software was used.” |
| **B** | “Random search – Hyperparameter values are randomly sampled from a predefined range to explore different configurations.” |
| **C** | “Grid search – All possible combinations of predefined hyperparameter values are systematically evaluated.” |
| **D** | “Model‑based optimization – Based on previous results, models are built and iteratively updated to predict performance and prioritize promising hyperparameter configurations.” |
| **E** | “Optimization heuristics – Rule‑based strategies iteratively searching for combinations of hyperparameters.” |
| **Y** | **Other** – The excerpt clearly indicates a tuning method that is **not** described by any of the definitions above. |
| **Z** | **Not reported** – The excerpt contains no information about the tuning method at all. |

**Instructions**  

1. **Read the excerpt carefully.**  
2. **Identify every tuning method that is explicitly mentioned** and that matches one of the definitions (A‑E).  
3. **If the excerpt mentions a method that does not fit any of A‑E, select Y (Other).**  
4. **If the excerpt mentions more than one applicable method, select **all** corresponding letters** (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
5. **If the excerpt provides no information about the tuning method, select only Z.**  
6. **Do not infer, guess, or add information that is not directly stated** in the excerpt.  
7. **Do not provide any explanation, commentary, or additional text**—only the final list of letters (or a single letter) in the exact format shown below.  

**Output Format**  

- A comma‑free, uppercase list of letters, sorted in alphabetical order if more than one (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
- If only one letter applies, output that single letter (e.g., `B`).  

**Reasoning (optional, for internal use only)**  
You may briefly note which sentence(s) in the excerpt support each chosen letter, but **do not include this reasoning in the final answer**.

**Example**  

```
Excerpt: “We performed a random search over learning‑rate and dropout values.”  
Answer: B
```

**Task:**  
Classify the excerpt from a research paper according to the predefined coding scheme for *Nested data structure*.

**Context:**  
You will be given a single excerpt (the *Excerpt*). Use only the information explicitly stated in that excerpt.

**Instructions (follow exactly):**

1. **Read the excerpt carefully.**  
2. **Choose the single option that best matches the description of the data used for *model training* (ignore any information that only refers to validation or test data).**  
3. **If the excerpt does not contain any information about a nested data structure for the training sample, respond with the letter **`Z`**.**  
4. **Do not infer, guess, or add information that is not directly mentioned.**  
5. **If the authors explicitly state that no nesting exists, choose option 0.**  
6. **If the authors describe a hierarchical arrangement (e.g., “patients nested within clinics”), choose option 1.**  
7. **If the authors describe repeated‑measure or longitudinal data where each row is a time‑point nested within a subject (long format), choose option 2.**  
8. **If the excerpt suggests a nested structure only by implication (e.g., the sample description hints at nesting but the authors never mention it), **do not** select an option; instead answer **`Z`**.**  
9. **When multiple nesting types are described, select the option that most directly matches the primary training data structure described.**  

**Output format:**  
Return **only** the selected answer (the digit `0`, `1`, `2`, or the letter `Z`) on a single line. No explanations, no additional text, no punctuation.

**Answer Options:**  
- `0` – No nested structure.  
- `1` – Hierarchical (e.g., patients nested in clinics).  
- `2` – Longitudinal / repeated measures (each row = a time point nested within a subject, long‑format data).  

**Excerpt:**  
{context}

**Answer:**

Do not provide any explanation or extra text. Target Variable: Handling of nested data structures Description: ;  Answer Options: A: Rationale why not necessary
B: Grouped / nested CV - Cross-validation methods that respect group structure (e.g., LGOCV), such that all observations from a group (e.g., patients of the same therapist) are confined to either the training or test fold.
C: Time series CV - For example, rolling / expanding window CV, blocked CV.
D: Mixed-effects ML algorithms - For example, applying regularized linear mixed-effect models (e.g., lmmlasso).

Target Variable: Handling of nested data structures

Answer Options:
A: Rationale why not necessary
B: Grouped / nested CV - Cross-validation methods that respect group structure (e.g., LGOCV), such that all observations from a group (e.g., patients of the same therapist) are confined to either the training or test fold.
C: Time series CV - For example, rolling / expanding window CV, blocked CV.
D: Mixed-effects ML algorithms - For example, applying regularized linear mixed-effect models (e.g., lmmlasso).

Answer:


**Task**  
You are a research‑assistant tasked with classifying information from a supplied excerpt of a research paper. Use **only** the information that appears explicitly in the excerpt.

**Context**  
{context}

**Target Variable**  
Handling of nested data structures  

**Answer Options**  

| Letter | Meaning |
|--------|--------------------------------------------------------------|
| A | Rationale why not necessary |
| B | Grouped / nested cross‑validation (e.g., LGOCV) that keeps all observations from a group together in either the training or test fold |
| C | Time‑series cross‑validation (e.g., rolling/expanding‑window, blocked CV) |
| D | Mixed‑effects machine‑learning algorithms (e.g., regularized linear mixed‑effect models such as *lmmlasso*) |
| Y | **Other** – the excerpt mentions a relevant approach that is **not** listed above |
| Z | **Not reported** – the excerpt contains no information about any of the above options |

**Instructions**  

1. **Read the excerpt carefully.** Identify every statement that **clearly** matches one of the answer options (A–D) or that describes a relevant approach not listed (choose Y).  
2. **Do not infer, guess, or add information** that is not explicitly stated in the excerpt.  
3. If the excerpt mentions **multiple distinct options**, select **all** applicable letters. List them in **alphabetical order**, separated by commas with **no spaces** (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
4. If the excerpt mentions a relevant approach that is **not** among A–D, select **Y** (Other). If both a listed option and an “Other” approach appear, include **both** letters.  
5. If the excerpt provides **no information** about any of the options, answer with **Z**.  
6. If the excerpt is ambiguous or vague such that a definitive match cannot be made, treat it as **not reported** and answer **Z**.  
7. **Output only the final letter(s).** Do not include explanations, headings, or any additional text.

**Output Format**  
A single line containing the selected letter(s) (e.g., `A,C` or `Z`).  

**Answer**:


Example 1:
Extract from paper: “To respect the hierarchical nature of the dataset, we applied leave‑group‑out cross‑validation (LGOCV), ensuring that all recordings from a given participant were kept together in either the training or the validation fold.” 
True Answer: B

**Task Overview**  
You are a research‑assistant tasked with extracting the *Sampling method applied* from a given excerpt of a research paper. Your response must consist **only** of the letter(s) that correspond to the correct answer(s) from the list below, formatted exactly as described.

---

### 1. Coding Scheme (Answer Options)

| Letter | Meaning |
|--------|---------|
| **A** | Up |
| **B** | Down |
| **C** | SMOTE |
| **Y** | Other (any sampling method mentioned that is **not** Up, Down, or SMOTE) |
| **Z** | None of the above – the excerpt contains **no information** about the sampling method |

---

### 2. Extraction Rules  

1. **Read the excerpt carefully.** Identify any explicit mention of a sampling method.  
2. **Match the mention to the coding scheme:**  
   - If the excerpt states **“Up”**, select **A**.  
   - If it states **“Down”**, select **B**.  
   - If it states **“SMOTE”**, select **C**.  
   - If it mentions any other sampling technique (e.g., “random oversampling”, “stratified sampling”, etc.), select **Y**.  
3. **Multiple methods:** If the excerpt lists *more than one* distinct sampling method, return **all applicable letters** separated by commas and no spaces (e.g., `A,C`). Preserve the order **A → B → C → Y** when multiple letters are needed.  
4. **No method mentioned:** If the excerpt contains **no reference** to any sampling method, answer **Z**.  
5. **Ambiguous or contradictory statements:**  
   - If the excerpt is unclear (e.g., “we used a sampling technique”) **without naming it**, treat it as **no information** → **Z**.  
   - If the excerpt contains conflicting methods (e.g., “both Up and SMOTE were applied”), include **all** relevant letters (`A,C`).  

---

### 3. Handling Missing or Unclear Information  

- **Do not infer** a method that is not explicitly named.  
- **Do not guess** based on context outside the excerpt.  
- If the excerpt mentions a method but the spelling or case differs (e.g., “up‑sampling”, “SMOTE‑based”), still map it to the appropriate letter (A, B, C, or Y) as long as the meaning is clear.  

---

### 4. Output Format  

- **Only** the letter(s) described above.  
- If multiple letters are required, separate them with a **single comma** and **no spaces** (e.g., `A,C,Y`).  
- If a single letter applies, output that single character (e.g., `B`).  
- **Never** include explanations, headings, or any additional text.

---

### 5. Reasoning (optional, for internal use)  

You may briefly verify the mapping internally before responding, but **do not** output any of that reasoning.  

---

You are an expert research assistant. From a given excerpt of a research paper you must identify which of the predefined comparison categories (A‑E) are **explicitly** mentioned. Return **only** the corresponding letter(s) in the format described below. Do **not** add explanations, comments, or any text that is not a letter code.

---

### 1. Source Material  
- **Excerpt:** `{context}` (the exact text you will analyze)  
- **Target variable:** *Model comparisons* – focus only on comparisons of predictive performance between models. Comparisons that are based solely on variable importance, feature selection, or any non‑predictive‑performance criteria must be ignored.

---

### 2. Answer Options (letter → definition)  

| Letter | Definition (must be stated **exactly** in the excerpt) |
|--------|--------------------------------------------------------|
| **A** | Comparison between a naïve guessing model (e.g., always predicts the majority class, random guessing, or a simple rule) and **any** machine‑learning (ML) model. |
| **B** | Comparison between an **unregularized** linear/logistic‑regression model and a **regularized** linear/logistic‑regression model **or** a more complex ML model. |
| **C** | Comparison **among** simple regularized linear/logistic models (Ridge, LASSO, Elastic‑Net). |
| **D** | Comparison between a **simple** regularized linear/logistic model **and** a **complex** ML model (any algorithm listed in section 3.1 of the source). |
| **E** | Comparison **among** different complex ML models. |
| **O** *(Other)* | The excerpt states a model‑comparison that is **clearly correct** but does **not** match any of the definitions A‑E. |
| **Z** | No information about any of the above comparisons is present in the excerpt. |

*Only the letters listed above may be output.*

---

### 3. Extraction Rules  

1. **Exact match required** – a category is selected **only** if the excerpt contains language that unambiguously satisfies the definition of that letter.  
2. **Multiple categories** – if more than one definition is satisfied, list **all** applicable letters.  
3. **Other (O)** – if the excerpt describes a valid model‑comparison that does not fit A‑E, add **O** to the output.  
4. **None (Z)** – if the excerpt contains **no** statement that matches any of A‑E or O, output only **Z**.  
5. **No inference** – do *not* infer or guess a comparison that is not explicitly stated.  
6. **No partial credit** – if a statement is ambiguous (e.g., “some models performed better”) and does not specify the type of comparison required, treat it as absent and do not select any letter for it.  

---

### 4. Output Format  

- **Single line** containing only the selected letter(s).  
- If more than one letter is needed, separate them with **commas, no spaces**, ordered alphabetically (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
- If only one letter applies, output just that letter (e.g., `D`).  
- **Never** include any additional words, punctuation (other than commas), or whitespace.

---

### 5. Optional Reasoning (internal)  

You may briefly reason internally to decide which letters apply, but **do not** include that reasoning in the final answer.

---

**Proceed now:**

Example: "Thirteen machine learning algorithms were selected to provide a 
broad comparison (of a variety of modelling techniques) and to be 
consistent with research that has used similar methods (Webb et al., 
2020). To provide a benchmark against the machine learning models, an 
ordinary least squares regression (OLS) model with all the available 
same predictors (in the same manner as the machine learning models) 
was included. The multiple OLS acts as a comparator with penalised 
regression models by fitting the data without any regularisation, making 
it prone to overfitting compared to models with regularisation. Addi
tionally, two other comparison models akin to those used in similar 
research were fitted (Buckman et al., 2021a). The first was an OLS 
regression model with only the pre-treatment GAD-7 score included, as 
initial symptom severity is one of the most important factors considered 
by clinicians in predicting treatment outcomes (and allocating treat
ment) for their patients (Amati et al., 2018; Buckman et al., 2021b; 
O’Driscoll et al., 2021). Separately, a null model was created using the 
mean post-treatment GAD-7 score in the training and validation dataset 
as the prediction for all test dataset cases. Five models based upon elastic 
net regularised regression (ENR; Friedman et al., 2010) were included 
(alpha parameters were 0.0; full ridge, 0.25, 0.50, 0.75 and 1.0; full 
lasso), using the ridge and least absolute shrinkage and selection oper
ator (lasso) penalisations to reduce overfitting. Two spline regression 
models were used: adaptive splines (Friedman, 1991) and adaptive 
polynomial splines (Stone et al., 1997). In addition, two 
decision-tree-based algorithms were used: random forest (Breiman, 
2001) and Bayesian Additive Regression Trees (BART; Chipman et al., 
2010). Lastly, three support vector machines (SVM) were included with 
linear, polynomial and radial kernels (Dimitriadou et al., 2009). Full 
details of the models are presented in Supplementary Material 1 
(Table 3). 
SuperLearner (van der Laan et al., 2007), the chosen ensemble 
modelling tool, uses cross-validation to estimate the predictive perfor
mance of individual machine learning algorithms, and ultimately de
termines the most accurate model within the ensemble." (Section 2.4; p.2)

**Task Overview**  
You are a research‑assistant tasked with extracting a single piece of information from a supplied excerpt of a research paper. The extraction must follow the coding rules below **exactly** and must not include any additional commentary, explanation, or invented text.

---

### 1. Target Variable  
**Best‑performing model (in the context of the authors’ comparison).**

### 2. Extraction Rules  

| Situation | What to output |
|-----------|----------------|
| **The excerpt names one specific algorithm/model as the best** | Output **exactly the name or phrase** used by the authors (copy‑paste, preserving spelling and capitalization). |
| **The authors state that more than one algorithm/model is best (i.e., no single algorithm outperforms the others)** | Output the code **`1`**. |
| **The authors claim an algorithm is best **but the reported performance numbers contradict this claim** (i.e., another algorithm clearly has higher scores)** | Output the code **`99`** **and** include a brief comment in parentheses stating *“contradicts reported metrics”*. Example: `99 (contradicts reported metrics)`. |
| **The excerpt does not contain any information about which model is best** | Output **`Z`**. |
| **The excerpt is ambiguous (e.g., it mentions “the top model” without naming it, or the wording could refer to different models)** | Treat this as “information not present” and output **`Z`**. |

*Do not guess, infer, or re‑phrase the information.*  
*Only use text that appears **exactly** in the excerpt (except for the coded values `1`, `99`, `Z`).*

---

### 3. Formatting Requirements  

- **Single line answer** only.  
- No extra spaces before or after the answer.  
- No explanatory text, headings, or punctuation other than what is required for the codes above.  

**Examples**

- Excerpt says: “Our experiments show that **BERT‑Large** consistently outperforms all baselines.” → `BERT‑Large`  
- Excerpt says: “Both **ResNet‑50** and **EfficientNet‑B0** achieve comparable results; no clear winner.” → `1`  
- Excerpt says: “We consider **XGBoost** the best model, even though **Random Forest** obtains higher accuracy.” → `99 (contradicts reported metrics)`  
- Excerpt contains no statement about the best model → `Z`  

---

### 4. Procedure  

1. **Read the provided excerpt** (the placeholder `{context}` will be replaced with the actual text).  
2. **Identify** whether any of the situations in the table apply.  
3. **Output** the appropriate answer **exactly** as specified.

Model performance is evaluated only on the training data.
B: Internal, no split - Model performance is assessed using cross-validation within the training data. No independent holdout set is used.
C: Internal, single split - The dataset is split once into a training and test set (e.g., 80/20). The model is trained on one portion and tested on the remaining portion.
D: Internal, repeated splits (i.e., some form of nested resampling) - Model performance is assessed using multiple train-test splits of the same dataset, meaning the initial data split is repeated multiple times (>1 iteration in the outer validation loop).
E: External, independent unseen holdout data, same population - Model is validated on a completely independent dataset, but from the same population as the training data (e.g., model is trained on US-participants of study 1 and validated on US-participants of study 2).

**Task**  
Classify the model‑validation approach described in a research‑paper excerpt according to the coding scheme below.

**Context**  
You will be given a single excerpt (the *Excerpt*). All decisions must be based **only** on the information explicitly present in that excerpt. Do not use any external knowledge, infer unstated details, or add commentary.

**Coding Scheme**  

| Letter | Meaning |
|--------|-----------------------------------------------------------|
| **A** | No validation – performance is reported only on the training data. |
| **B** | Internal, no split – performance is assessed by cross‑validation within the training set; no independent hold‑out set. |
| **C** | Internal, single split – one train‑test split (e.g., 80/20) is used. |
| **D** | Internal, repeated splits – multiple train‑test splits or nested resampling (>1 outer iteration). |
| **E** | External, same population – validation on an independent data set drawn from the same population as the training data. |
| **F** | External, different population – validation on an independent data set from a different population. |
| **O** | **Other** – the excerpt clearly indicates a validation method that does **not** match any of the letters A‑F. |
| **Z** | Not reported – the excerpt contains no information about model validation. |

**Instructions**  

1. **Read the excerpt carefully.** Identify every validation approach that is *explicitly* described.  
2. **Select all applicable letters** (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
   - If the excerpt mentions more than one distinct approach, list every corresponding letter.  
   - If the described approach matches none of A‑F, return the single letter **O**.  
3. **If the excerpt provides no validation information at all**, return **Z**.  
4. **Do not guess, infer, or extrapolate** beyond what is written.  
5. **Do not provide explanations, rationales, or any additional text**—only the final list of letters (or a single letter).  

**Edge‑Case Handling**  

- **Ambiguous wording**: If the wording could be interpreted as more than one category, choose the *most specific* category that is unambiguously supported. If ambiguity remains, treat the information as not reported and answer **Z**.  
- **Partial information**: If the excerpt mentions only part of a validation procedure (e.g., “we used cross‑validation”) without specifying split type, select the letter that matches the stated part (e.g., **B**).  
- **Multiple validation stages**: If both internal and external validation are described, include letters for each stage (e.g., `B,F`).  

**Output Format**  

- A comma‑separated list of capital letters with **no spaces** (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
- If only one letter applies, output that single letter (e.g., `Z`).  

**Example**  

```
Excerpt: “The model was trained on the 2018 cohort and then tested on the 2019 cohort, which includes participants from a different country.”
Answer: F
```

Answer:


**Task Overview**  
You are an expert research assistant. From the excerpt provided, you must determine how the authors handled **data‑preparation steps for the training set versus the test set** and code the answer according to the scheme below.

---

### 1. What you must decide  

- **Jointly** – the same preparation step(s) were applied to *both* the training and test data at the same time.  
- **Separately** – the preparation step(s) were applied to the training data **and** to the test data **independently** (i.e., the test data were never used to influence the training‑set processing).  

If the excerpt does not mention any data‑preparation step for the training or test set, or if it is impossible to tell from the text, you must indicate that the information is **not reported**.

If the study does **not** use a validation split (e.g., “no validation”, “internal study, no split”), the variable is **not applicable**.

---

### 2. Coding scheme (choose **exactly one**)

| Code | Meaning |
|------|---------|
| **0** | Not reported – the excerpt gives no information about whether preparation was joint or separate. |
| **1** | Jointly – it is explicitly stated that a preparation step was performed *jointly* on training + test data. |
| **2** | Separately – it is explicitly stated that preparation steps were performed *separately* on training and test data. |
| **-99** | Not applicable – the study has “no validation” or “internal, no split” as described in section 4.2. |

*Important:* **Only** code **1** when the document **clearly** says the step was performed jointly. Do **not** infer joint processing from the order in which methods are described or from any chronological narrative.

---

### 3. Handling special cases  

- **Missing information** – If the excerpt provides no mention of training‑test preparation, return **0**.  
- **Ambiguous or contradictory statements** – If you cannot determine a single, unambiguous answer, also return **0**.  
- **No validation / no split** – If the excerpt contains a phrase such as “no validation”, “internal study, no split”, or any equivalent wording indicating that a test set was never created, return **-99**.  
- **Multiple preparation steps** – If some steps are joint *and* others are separate, follow the rule in the original description: code as **1 (Jointly)** **only** when it is *clearly* stated that a step was performed jointly. Otherwise, treat the overall variable as **0 (Not reported)** because the information is mixed/unclear.

---

### 4. Output format  

- Return **only the numeric code** (0, 1, 2, or -99).  
- Do **not** include any explanation, label, or additional characters.  
- Example of a correct response: `2`

---

### 5. Reasoning (optional, for internal use only)  

You may briefly note to yourself why you chose a particular code, but **do not** output that reasoning. It is only for your internal verification.

---

### 6. Prompt template  

```
Excerpt:
{context}

Your answer:
```

You are an expert research assistant. From the supplied excerpt, you must assign **exactly one code** that best represents how the authors treat model comparison, according to the coding scheme below.

**Output Requirement**  
- Return **only the code** (e.g., `1`, `2`, `Z`, or `-99`).  
- No additional words, symbols, explanations, or whitespace are allowed.

**Coding Scheme**  

| Code | Meaning (must be supported by the excerpt) |
|------|--------------------------------------------|
| **1** | **Descriptive comparison** – the authors mention or show model performance differences **without providing an explanatory interpretation** (e.g., “Random forest achieved 85 % accuracy, elastic net 78 %”). |
| **2** | **Interpretative comparison** – the authors **draw a conclusion or hypothesis** about why one model outperforms another or what the comparison reveals about the data (e.g., “The superior performance of the random forest suggests the presence of interaction effects”). |
| **Z** | The excerpt **contains no information** about model comparison (no mention of multiple models, performance metrics, or any comparison). |
| **-99** | The target variable is **not applicable** because the prerequisite condition “0 in 4.1” is true. *(If you do not have that prerequisite information, ignore this code and follow the other rules.)* |

**Decision Procedure**  

1. **Search the excerpt** for any reference to **two or more predictive models** (e.g., random forest, elastic net, SVM, etc.) and any **performance metric** or result that links them.  
2. **Classify the nature of the comparison**:  
   - If the text only **reports numbers or statements** about which model performed better **without explaining why**, select **1**.  
   - If the text **provides an explanation, hypothesis, or inference** about the reason for the performance difference or what it implies about the data, select **2**.  
3. **If both types appear**, the interpretative element supersedes the descriptive one; choose **2**.  
4. **If no model‑comparison content is found**, output **Z**.  
5. **If you know that “0 in 4.1” applies**, output **-99** regardless of the excerpt content.  

**Handling Ambiguity & Missing Information**  

- **Do not infer** missing details. If the excerpt is vague (e.g., “Model A performed better”) and provides no explanatory reason, treat it as **descriptive** (code 1).  
- If the excerpt mentions only a single model or only general performance without a comparative reference, treat it as **no comparison** (code Z).  
- If the excerpt contains contradictory statements, prioritize the **most explicit** statement present.  

**Optional Reasoning (for your internal use only)**  
You may silently note why you chose a particular code to improve accuracy, but **do not include that reasoning in the final answer**.

---

3. **Select the corresponding letter(s).**  
   - If more than one language meets the criteria, list all applicable letters separated by commas (e.g., `A,B`).

4. **Do not** infer, guess, or add any interpretation beyond the excerpt.

**Output format**  
Exactly one line containing the selected letter(s) (e.g., `A`, `A,B,E`, `Y`, or `Z`). No surrounding punctuation or commentary.

**Example**

*Answer:* `A` (because both packages are exclusive to R)

**Target Variable:** *Programming language version reported*  

**Definition:**  
- **Yes** – The authors explicitly state the exact version of the programming language they used (e.g., “Python 3.9”, “R 4.2.1”).  
- **No** – The excerpt mentions a programming language but does **not** give a specific version.  
- **Missing** – The excerpt contains no reference to any programming language at all.

**Answer Options (choose **exactly one**):**  
1 – No (language mentioned, version not given)  
2 – Yes (exact version is given)  
Z – Missing (no language information present)

**Instructions:**  

1. **Read the excerpt carefully.**  
2. **Identify** whether a programming language is mentioned and, if so, whether a precise version number is provided.  
3. **Select the appropriate option** (1, 2, or Z) **based strictly on the text**.  
4. **Do not**:  
   - Infer a version from context or external knowledge.  
   - Guess when the information is ambiguous.  
   - Provide any explanation, commentary, or additional characters.  
5. **If the excerpt is ambiguous** (e.g., it says “Python” without a version, or the version is described only qualitatively such as “latest” or “current”), treat it as **No** (option 1).  
6. **If the excerpt contains no mention of a programming language**, answer **Z**.  

**Output format:**

**Task Overview**  
You are an expert research assistant. Your job is to scan the supplied excerpt and pull out **exactly** the names of machine‑learning packages, libraries, or modeling frameworks that appear in the text. Follow the coding scheme below without adding, guessing, or interpreting anything that is not explicitly written in the excerpt.

---

### Instructions  

1. **Read the excerpt** (provided after the line `Excerpt:`).  
2. **Identify every distinct term** that refers to a ML package, library, or modeling framework.  
   - Use the **exact spelling and capitalization** as it appears in the excerpt.  
   - If a term is mentioned more than once, list it **only once** (first occurrence order).  
3. **Output format**  
   - Return the result **as a plain list** (e.g., `["TensorFlow", "scikit‑learn"]`).  
   - If **no such term** is present, output the single token `Z` (without brackets).  
4. **Do not**:  
   - Invent or infer names that are not explicitly in the excerpt.  
   - Provide explanations, commentary, or any text other than the list (or `Z`).  
   - Alter the spelling, add abbreviations, or merge separate terms.  
5. **Edge‑case handling**  
   - If a term is ambiguous (e.g., a partial name or a common word that could be a package), **include it only if the excerpt clearly presents it as a ML package/library/framework**.  
   - If the excerpt contains a citation or parenthetical reference that includes a package name, treat the name as valid and extract it.  
   - If a term is hyphenated, contains special characters, or is a versioned name, keep the full token exactly as shown.  

---

### Coding Scheme  

- **Target Variable:** `ML packages / libraries / modeling frameworks mentioned`

```

**Task Overview**  
You are a research‑assistant tasked with coding a single piece of information from a supplied excerpt of a research paper. The coding follows a fixed scheme; only one code may be chosen for each excerpt.

---

### 1. Coding Target  
**Variable:** *Package / library / modeling framework version reported*  

**Definition:**  
- **“Yes”** – The authors **explicitly** list the exact version number(s) of any machine‑learning‑related software (e.g., “scikit‑learn 1.2.2”, “caret 6.0‑93”, “mlr3 0.15.0”).  
- **“No”** – No version numbers are mentioned.  
- **“Z”** – The excerpt does not contain any information about software packages, libraries, or modeling frameworks (i.e., the target is completely absent).

---

### 2. Answer Options (choose **exactly one**)  

| Code | Meaning |
|------|---------|
| **1** | No – the excerpt discusses software but does **not** provide version numbers. |
| **2** | Yes – the excerpt **explicitly** reports version numbers of ML‑related packages, libraries, or meta‑modeling frameworks. |
| **Z** | Not applicable – the excerpt contains **no** reference to any package, library, or modeling framework at all. |

---

### 3. Instructions (strictly followed)

1. **Read the excerpt** (the text appearing after `Excerpt:`) **carefully**.  
2. **Identify** whether the excerpt mentions any ML‑related package, library, or modeling framework **and** whether a **specific version number** is provided.  
3. **Select the single appropriate code** from the table above:
   - If a version number is present → **2**.  
   - If the software is mentioned **without** a version → **1**.  
   - If there is **no mention** of software at all → **Z**.  
4. **Do not infer** missing information, extrapolate from other sections, or use external knowledge.  
5. **Do not provide any explanation, justification, or additional text**—output **only** the chosen code (e.g., `2`).  
6. **Edge‑case handling**:  
   - If multiple packages are listed and **any one** includes an explicit version, the correct code is **2**.  
   - If the excerpt contains ambiguous phrasing such as “using the latest version” or “version X.Y.Z (unspecified)”, treat this as **no explicit version** → **1**.  
   - If the excerpt is empty or consists solely of non‑technical filler, answer **Z**.  

---

### 4. Optional Reasoning (for internal verification only)  
*You may mentally note the justification for your choice, but **do not** include it in the final output.*

---

### 5. Input Format  

```
Excerpt:
{context}
Answer:
```

Select exactly one number that best applies, based strictly on the provided document.

Target Variable: Open data
Description: ; 
Answer Options: 0: Not available - Original or preprocessed data are either not mentioned or not available, and no information is provided on how they can be accessed (e.g., the repository is private, the link is broken, or the resource is otherwise inaccessible).
1: Available upon request - Original or preprocessed data are available, but only by directly requesting them from the authors.
2: Synthetic data only - Only synthetic or simulated data are provided; original or preprocessed data are not available.
3: Public Repository (restricted or open) - Original or preprocessed data is deposited in a public repository; access may require registration or agreement.
4: Fully open access - Data are openly available via supplemental materials or a direct repository link, with no restrictions.

Answer Options: 0: Not available - Original or preprocessed data are either not mentioned or not available, and no information is provided on how they can be accessed (e.g., the repository is private, the link is broken, or the resource is otherwise inaccessible).
1: Available upon request - Original or preprocessed data are available, but only by directly requesting them from the authors.
2: Synthetic data only - Only synthetic or simulated data are provided; original or preprocessed data are not available.
3: Public Repository (restricted or open) - Original or preprocessed data is deposited in a public repository; access may require registration or agreement.
4: Fully open access - Data are openly available via supplemental materials or a direct repository link, with no restrictions.

{context}
Answer:


**Task Overview**  
You are an expert research assistant. Your job is to read a short excerpt from a research paper and assign a single code that best describes the accessibility of the data reported in that excerpt, according to the coding scheme below.

**Input Provided**  
- **Excerpt:** `{context}` (the text you must analyze)  
- **Target Variable:** *Open data*  

**Output Requirement**  
- Return **exactly one character**: either the numeric code (0‑4) that matches the excerpt **or** the letter **Z** if the excerpt provides no information about data availability.  
- **Do not** include any additional text, explanations, symbols, or whitespace.  

**Coding Scheme**  

| Code | Meaning (must match the excerpt *exactly*) |
|------|--------------------------------------------|
| **0** | *Not available* – The excerpt mentions no data, states the data are unavailable, or gives no access details (e.g., private repository, broken link). |
| **1** | *Available upon request* – The excerpt says the data can be obtained only by contacting the authors. |
| **2** | *Synthetic data only* – The excerpt indicates that only simulated or synthetic data are provided; no real/original data are shared. |
| **3** | *Public repository (restricted or open)* – The excerpt notes that the data are deposited in a public repository, even if access requires registration, an agreement, or similar restrictions. |
| **4** | *Fully open access* – The excerpt states that the data are freely downloadable (e.g., supplemental files or a direct repository link) with **no** restrictions. |
| **Z** | *No information* – The excerpt does not mention data availability at all. |

**Step‑by‑Step Procedure**  

1. **Read the excerpt carefully.**  
2. **Identify any statement about data availability** (e.g., “data are available…”, “can be obtained by contacting…”, “synthetic data only”, “deposited in…”, “downloadable at…”).  
3. **Match the identified statement to the most specific code** in the table above.  
   - If multiple statements appear, choose the code that reflects the *most permissive* access described (e.g., if both “available upon request” and “deposited in a public repository” are mentioned, select **3**).  
4. **If the excerpt contains no mention of data at all**, output **Z**.  
5. **If the excerpt is ambiguous or contradictory**, apply the following hierarchy to resolve it (higher number = more permissive): 0 → 1 → 2 → 3 → 4. Choose the highest‑ranking code that can be justified.  
6. **Output only the selected code** (0‑4 or Z) with no trailing newline or spaces.

**Important Rules**  

- **Do not infer** information that is not explicitly stated.  
- **Do not guess** or provide any rationale.  
- **Do not add** any characters other than the single required output.  

**Example**  

```
Excerpt: "All raw sequencing files are deposited in the NCBI SRA under accession XYZ and are freely downloadable."
Answer: 4
```

**Task Overview**  
You are an expert research assistant. Your job is to read a short excerpt from a research paper and assign a single code that indicates whether the study was *preregistered* according to the excerpt.

**Coding Scheme (choose **exactly one** option)**  

| Code | Meaning |
|------|----------|
| **1** | The excerpt states **explicitly** that the study was *not* preregistered, or it provides a clear statement that no preregistration was performed. |
| **2** | The excerpt states **explicitly** that the study *was* preregistered (e.g., mentions a registration number, a registry, a protocol, or any definitive confirmation of preregistration). |
| **Z** | The excerpt contains **no information** about preregistration – the topic is not mentioned, the statement is ambiguous, or the excerpt is silent on the matter. |

**Instructions – Follow These Steps Rigorously**

1. **Read the entire excerpt** (the text that replaces `{context}`) carefully.  
2. **Search for any explicit statement** about preregistration:
   - Look for phrases such as “preregistered”, “registered”, “pre‑registered”, “protocol was deposited”, “registered on ClinicalTrials.gov”, “OSF registration #…”, etc.
   - A statement that the study was *not* preregistered (e.g., “the study was not preregistered”, “no preregistration was performed”) qualifies for code **1**.
3. **If an explicit confirmation of preregistration is found**, assign code **2**.  
4. **If the excerpt does not mention preregistration at all**, or the mention is vague/uncertain (e.g., “we considered preregistration”, “preregistration may have been done”, “the protocol was prepared”), assign code **Z**. Do **not** infer or guess based on external knowledge.  
5. **If contradictory statements appear** (both “preregistered” and “not preregistered”), prioritize the **most explicit** claim. If they are truly ambiguous, default to **Z**.  
6. **Output** **only** the single code (`1`, `2`, or `Z`). **No additional text, explanations, or formatting** is allowed.

**Optional Reasoning (for internal verification only – do **not** include it in the final answer)**  
- After deciding on a code, you may briefly note to yourself which exact sentence led to the decision. This reasoning should stay private and must not appear in the response.

**Task Overview**  
You are an expert research assistant. Your job is to read a supplied excerpt from a research paper and determine the **Country (or countries) where the data were collected**. You must then encode this information using the predefined coding scheme below.

---

### 1. Source‑only Requirement  
- **Base every decision exclusively on the information that appears in the excerpt.**  
- Do **not** infer, guess, or add any knowledge that is not explicitly stated.  
- Do **not** provide explanations, comments, or any text other than the final answer.

### 2. Mapping the Country  
| Letter | Country (or grouping) |
|--------|-----------------------|
| **A** | Cross‑cultural (specific countries not listed) |
| **B** | USA |
| **C** | UK |
| **D** | Germany |
| **E** | Australia |
| **F** | Canada |
| **G** | China |
| **H** | Netherlands |
| **I** | France |
| **J** | Italy |
| **K** | Brazil |
| **L** | Spain |
| **M** | Japan |
| **N** | Turkey |
| **O** | South Korea |
| **P** | Sweden |
| **Q** | Switzerland |
| **R** | Israel |
| **S** | Norway |
| **Y** | **Other** (any country not listed, or a region/continent that cannot be mapped to a specific listed country) |
| **Z** | Not reported (no country, city, state, region, or any geographic cue is present) |

### 3. How to Choose the Letter(s)

1. **Exact country name** – If the excerpt names a country that appears in the table, output the corresponding letter.  
2. **City/State/Region → Country** – If only a city, state, province, or region is mentioned, map it to its country **provided the mapping is unambiguous** (e.g., “Toronto” → Canada). Use the appropriate letter.  
3. **Multiple countries** – If the excerpt clearly indicates data from more than one distinct country, list **all applicable letters** separated by commas, in **alphabetical order** (e.g., `B,D,G`).  
4. **Cross‑cultural design** – If the study explicitly states it is “cross‑cultural” or “multinational” **without listing the specific countries**, output **A**.  
5. **Other country** – If the country is mentioned but it is **not** in the table (e.g., “India”), output **Y**.  
6. **Only a continent/region** – If the excerpt refers only to a continent or a vague region (e.g., “Europe”, “Southeast Asia”) and no specific country can be derived, output **Y**.  
7. **Ambiguous city/region** – If a place name could belong to more than one country (e.g., “Springfield”) and the excerpt does not clarify which one, treat this as **Z** (Not reported).  
8. **No geographic information** – If the excerpt contains no reference to a country, city, state, or region, output **Z**.  

### 4. Output Format  

- Return **only** the letter(s) described above.  
- When multiple letters are required, separate them with a **single comma** and **no spaces** (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
- If a single letter is needed, return it alone (e.g., `B`).  

### 5. Optional Reasoning (Do Not Include in Output)  

If you wish to improve accuracy, you may internally follow this checklist before producing the final answer:  

1. Scan the excerpt for any country name → map to letter.  
2. If none, scan for city/state/region → map unambiguously to a country.  
3. If still none, check for “cross‑cultural” wording.  
4. If still none, check for any non‑listed country or vague region → Y.  
5. If nothing at all → Z.  

**Do not** output this reasoning; it is for internal use only.

---

**Task**  
You are a research assistant who must code each excerpt according to a fixed taxonomy of predictor‑variable data types. Follow the steps exactly and output **only** the required letters (or “Z”)—no explanations, no extra text.

---

### Procedure  

1. **Read the excerpt** (the text that follows the label **“Excerpt:”**).  
2. **Identify every data type that is explicitly mentioned** in the excerpt.  
   - A data type matches only when the passage unambiguously describes it; do **not** infer from context or from outside knowledge.  
3. **Select the corresponding letter(s)** from the list below:  
   - List **all applicable letters** in **alphabetical order**, separated by commas **without spaces** (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
   - If the excerpt states a data type **not listed** among the options, also select the letter **`O`** (designated for “Other”).  
   - If **no information** about any listed data type is present, answer **`Z`**.  
4. **Do not provide any additional text** (no reasoning, no commentary). The final answer must be exactly the letter(s) or `Z`.

---

### Handling Ambiguity & Missing Information  

- **Ambiguous statements** (e.g., “some physiological measure” without a clear definition) are treated as **not present**; do not select a letter.  
- **Partial mentions** that do not match any definition verbatim are also considered **absent**.  
- If a single sentence could belong to more than one category but is not **clearly** defined, **do not** select any letter for that sentence.  

---

### Answer Options (letter → definition)  

- **A:** Behavioral observation data – systematic recordings or codings of observable actions or interactions (e.g., classroom participation, gaze behavior, social engagement).  
- **B:** Performance data – outcomes from task‑based assessments (e.g., IQ scores, reaction time, cognitive‑task results).  
- **C:** Digital trace data – automatically logged behavioral data from digital devices or platforms (e.g., screen time, app usage, GPS location, typing dynamics).  
- **D:** Higher‑dimensional data from text / image / audio – features extracted from unstructured modalities such as natural language, visual input, or sound (e.g., text embeddings, pixel‑based image features, audio spectrograms).  
- **E:** Biophysical measures – quantitative measurements of biological or physiological structures or functions (e.g., genetic markers, hormone levels, heart‑rate variability).  
- **F:** Rating / judgement data – structured evaluations based on rating scales or subjective judgments (e.g., Likert‑scale responses, observer ratings).  
- **G:** Demographic / biographical data – factual records about an individual’s life circumstances or traits (e.g., age, marital status, education, medical diagnoses).  
- **H:** Contextual / aggregate data – features of the broader environment or context (e.g., regional average income, school‑level teacher‑student ratios, therapist gender).  
- **O:** Other – the excerpt explicitly mentions a data type that does **not** fit any of A‑H.  

---

**Task**: From the excerpt (provided in the placeholder **{context}**) select **all** letters that **explicitly** describe the data type of the outcome variable. Follow the rules exactly; do not add any explanation, commentary, or characters beyond the required letters.

**Coding Scheme**  

| Letter | Category (definition) |
|--------|-----------------------|
| A | Behavioral observation data – systematic recordings or codings of observable actions or interactions (e.g., classroom participation, gaze behavior, social engagement). |
| B | Performance data – task‑based or ability‑driven assessments that yield quantifiable outcomes (e.g., IQ scores, reaction time, cognitive‑task results). |
| C | Digital trace data – automatically logged behavioral data from digital devices or platforms, passively collected (e.g., screen‑time, app usage, GPS location, typing dynamics). |
| D | Higher‑dimensional data from text / image / audio – features extracted from unstructured modalities (e.g., text embeddings, pixel‑based image features, audio spectrograms). |
| E | Biophysical measures – quantitative measurements of biological or physiological structures or functions (e.g., genetic markers, hormone levels, heart‑rate variability). |
| F | Rating / judgement data – structured evaluations based on rating scales or subjective judgments (e.g., Likert‑scale responses, observer ratings). |
| G | Demographic / biographical data – factual or historical records about an individual’s life circumstances or traits (e.g., age, marital status, education history, medical diagnoses). |
| H | Contextual / aggregate data – features of the broader environment or context (e.g., regional average income, school‑level teacher‑student ratios, therapist gender in multilevel data). |
| O | **Other** – the excerpt clearly indicates a data type that is **not** captured by A‑H. |
| Z | **None** – the excerpt contains no information that matches any of the categories A‑H (or is ambiguous such that no definitive category can be assigned). |

**Instructions**  

1. **Read only the excerpt** (the text inside **{context}**). Do not use any external knowledge.  
2. Identify every category that is **explicitly stated** in the excerpt. Implicit inference or guesswork is not allowed.

6. **Do not** provide any rationale, explanation, or additional text. The output must be **exactly** the letters (or `Z`) as specified.

**Task Overview**  
You will read a short excerpt from a research paper and assign it to one category of a predefined coding scheme.  

**Output Requirement**  
- Return **exactly one token**: either `1`, `2`, or the letter `Z`.  
- **Do not** include any additional characters, spaces, line‑breaks, or explanations.  

**When to use each token**  

| Token | Meaning (must match the excerpt *exactly*) |
|------|----------------------------------------------|
| **1** | The outcome is already known or measured at the same time as (or before) the predictor data. Example: “predicting personality traits with music‑listening behavior.” |
| **2** | The outcome occurs **after** the predictor data were collected and is therefore unknown at that point. Example: “predicting relapse or therapy dropout using predictors gathered at the start of therapy.” |
| **Z** | The excerpt provides **no information** that allows you to determine whether the outcome is contemporaneous or future‑oriented. In other words, the required information is missing, ambiguous, or irrelevant. |

**Guidelines for Classification**  

1. **Strictly follow the excerpt** – base your decision only on the text supplied in the *Excerpt* field.  
2. **Do not infer or guess** – if the excerpt does not explicitly state the temporal relationship between predictor and outcome, choose `Z`.  
3. **Edge‑case handling**  
   - If the excerpt mentions both a contemporaneous and a future outcome, select the option that **best matches the primary outcome** described.  
   - If the excerpt is vague (e.g., “we will examine the relationship between X and Y”) and does not specify timing, answer `Z`.  
4. **No reasoning required** – you may internally reason to reach the correct token, but **do not output** that reasoning.

```

Answer:


**Task Overview**  
You are a research‑assistant tasked with **classifying a single excerpt from a research paper** according to a fixed coding scheme that describes how the authors report missing data.

**Input Provided**  
- **Target Variable:** *Reporting of missingness*  
- **Answer Options:**  

| Code | Meaning (exactly as defined) |
|------|--------------------------------|
| **0** | *Not reported* – The amount of missing data is **not described at all**. |
| **1** | *General statement* – The authors give a **qualitative** description (e.g., “low missingness”) **or** a **single quantitative summary** that applies to the whole sample (e.g., “5 % missing overall”). |
| **2** | *Descriptives on variable level* – The authors **quantify missing values for each variable** (e.g., “Variable X: 3 % missing, Variable Y: 12 % missing”). |
| **Z** | *Information absent* – The excerpt provides **no information** about missing data (i.e., none of the definitions above apply). |

- **Excerpt:** `{context}` (replace with the actual text you are classifying)

**Instructions – What to Do**  

1. **Read the excerpt carefully.**  
2. **Determine which single code (0, 1, 2, or Z) best matches the excerpt** according to the definitions above.  
   - If **multiple definitions appear**, select the **most specific** applicable code (the hierarchy is 0 < 1 < 2).

3. **Do not infer or guess** any information that is not explicitly stated.  
4. **If the excerpt contains no mention of missing data at all**, answer **Z**.  
5. **Output Requirement:** Return **only the single code** (0, 1, 2, or Z) **with no additional text, explanation, or formatting**.

Answer Options: A: Listwise deletion - An observation is excluded from the analysis if it has any missing values on the variables included, meaning only complete cases are analyzed.
B: Threshold-based deletion - A variable or observation is excluded from the analysis if the proportion of missing data exceeded a predefined threshold (e.g., if more than 50% of its values were missing).

**Task:**  
Classify the handling‑of‑missing‑data method(s) described in a research‑paper excerpt according to the coding scheme below.

**Instructions – follow them exactly:**

1. **Read the excerpt only** (the text between `Excerpt:` and `Answer:`). Do **not** consult any external files, code, or supplementary material unless the authors explicitly refer to those materials *within the excerpt*.

2. **Select every letter that matches a method that is **clearly stated** in the excerpt.**  
   - If the excerpt mentions a method that is **not** listed among the answer options, also select **Y (Other)**.  
   - If **more than one** method is mentioned, list all applicable letters **in alphabetical order**, separated by commas (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
   - If **no information** about missing‑data handling is present, answer **only** `Z`.  
   - **Never** guess, infer, or add any explanation.

3. **Missing or ambiguous information:**  
   - If a statement is vague (e.g., “missing values were dealt with appropriately”) and does not specify a concrete method, treat it as **not reported** → answer `Z`.  
   - If a method is described partially but the essential technique is identifiable (e.g., “we removed cases with any missing values”), treat it as the corresponding code (here, `A`).  
   - If the excerpt refers to a supplementary file for details **without describing the method itself**, treat the method as **not reported** → answer `Z`.

4. **Output format:**  
   - Provide **only** the selected letter(s) (or `Z`) on a single line, with no surrounding text, punctuation other than commas, or whitespace.  
   - Example of a valid answer: `A,C,E` or `Z`.

---

**Target Variable:** Handling of missing data  

**Answer Options (choose exactly one or more letters):**  

- **A:** Listwise deletion – an observation is excluded if it has any missing values on the variables used (complete‑case analysis).  
- **B:** Threshold‑based deletion – a variable or observation is excluded when the proportion of missing data exceeds a predefined threshold (e.g., > 50 %).  
- **C:** Mean / median / mode imputation – missing values are replaced by a single central‑tendency statistic.  
- **D:** k‑nearest neighbors (k‑NN) imputation – missing values are imputed using the values of the *k* nearest observations.  
- **E:** Multiple imputation (specify algorithm and number of datasets *m* in a comment) – missing values are imputed multiple times to create several complete datasets.  
- **Y:** Other – any explicitly described method that does not fit A‑E.  
- **Z:** Not reported – the excerpt provides no information about how missing data were handled.

**Task Overview**

**Target Variable**  
*Feature enrichment* – the use of domain knowledge to create new or more informative variables (e.g., aggregating items, computing indices, adding external data, etc.).

**Coding Scheme (choose all that apply)**  

| Letter | Definition (exact wording to match) |
|--------|--------------------------------------|
| **A** | *Aggregating items to scales* – “Items of the PHQ‑9 are combined into a single scale score by summing / averaging the item responses.” |
| **B** | *Clustering (grouping raw data to features)* – “Answers in a structured interview are defined as a diagnosis based on DSM‑5 criteria.” |
| **C** | *Calculation of indices* – “Deriving common indices, such as BMI, using standardized formulas from variables like weight and height.” |
| **D** | *Pre‑specifying relations between predictors* – “Interactions or quadratic terms of predictors are computed.” |
| **E** | *Computing latent trait scores* – “A score is computed for items of an underlying latent construct based on observed data, such as factor scores or Item Response Theory (IRT) scores.” |
| **F** | *Enrichment via external data sources* – “Integrating relevant external data to provide additional context or meaningful information (e.g., adding location data of cafés, gyms, and libraries to individuals’ GPS movement data).” |
| **G** | *Extracting features from text / image / audio data* – (any description of automatic extraction from unstructured media). |
| **Y** | **Other** – the excerpt mentions a valid feature‑enrichment method **not listed** above. |
| **Z** | **Not reported** – the excerpt contains **no** information about any feature‑enrichment method. |

**Instructions for Selecting Letters**

1. **Base your decision ONLY on the excerpt** provided. Do **not** infer, guess, or use outside knowledge.  
2. **Match the excerpt verbatim** (or paraphrase that preserves the meaning) to the definitions above. If the excerpt clearly describes a method that fits a definition, select that letter.  
3. **Multiple methods:** If more than one definition applies, list **all applicable letters** in **alphabetical order**, separated by commas **without spaces** (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
4. **Other:** If the excerpt describes a legitimate feature‑enrichment technique that does **not** correspond to any letter A‑G, select **Y** **in addition** to any other applicable letters.  
5. **Not reported:** If the excerpt contains **no** mention of any feature‑enrichment technique, output only `Z`.  
6. **Ambiguous or partial information:**  
   - If the excerpt is vague such that it could plausibly fit a definition but is **not explicitly stated**, treat it as **not reported** and answer `Z`.  
   - If the excerpt contains contradictory statements, prioritize the **explicitly stated** method(s) and ignore the contradictory part.  
7. **Output format:** Provide **exactly one line** containing only the selected letter(s) (or `Z`). Do **not** add explanations, headings, or any other text.

**Optional Reasoning (for internal use only)**  
If you find it helpful, you may briefly note which part of the excerpt matched each selected letter before producing the final answer, but **do not include that reasoning in the output**.

**Task**  
You are a research‑assistant tasked with **coding** a short excerpt from a research paper. The excerpt must be examined **strictly** for evidence of any of the listed feature‑engineering techniques.  

**Target variable**: *Feature engineering*  

**Coding scheme (answer letters)**  

| Letter | Technique (select if the excerpt explicitly reports this) |
|--------|-----------------------------------------------------------|
| **A** | Standardization (z‑scores) |
| **B** | Centering |
| **C** | Min‑max scaling |
| **D** | Logarithmic transformation |
| **E** | Dimensionality reduction – if reported, note the specific method (e.g., PCA) in a **comment** after the answer list |
| **F** | Categorization – conversion of continuous/ordinal variables into categories (e.g., cut‑off scores) |
| **G** | Winsorization of outliers – extreme values are capped/replaced |
| **H** | Outlier masking + imputation – outliers first treated as missing, then imputed |
| **Y** | **Other** – the excerpt mentions a valid feature‑engineering step that is **not** listed above |
| **Z** | Not reported – the excerpt contains **no** information about any of the techniques above |

**Input**  
- **Excerpt**: `{context}` (the exact text to be evaluated)  

**Instructions**

3. **If the excerpt mentions a technique that does not appear in the table,** add the letter **Y** (Other).  
4. **If multiple techniques are reported,** list all applicable letters **in alphabetical order**, separated by commas (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
5. **If the excerpt provides no information about any technique,** output **only** the letter `Z`.  
6. **If you select letter E (Dimensionality reduction),** you must add a short comment in parentheses after the answer list indicating the specific method mentioned (e.g., `E (PCA)`). No comment is required for any other letter.  
7. **If the excerpt is ambiguous** (e.g., it hints at a technique but does not name it), treat it as *not reported* and answer `Z`.  
8. **Do not provide any explanation, rationale, or additional text** beyond the answer letters (and the optional comment for E).  

**Output format**  

- A single line containing only the selected letter(s) (and optional comment for E).  
- Examples:  
  - `A,B,F`  
  - `E (PCA),G`

**Task Overview**  
You are an expert research assistant. Your job is to read a short excerpt from a research paper and indicate which machine‑learning algorithms (from the list below) are **explicitly mentioned** as being used for prediction.  

**Output Format**  
- Return **only** the capital letters that correspond to the applicable answer options, separated by commas and **without any spaces** (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
- If **no algorithm from the list** is mentioned, output the single letter `Z`.  
- If the excerpt mentions a correct algorithm **that is not** in the list, include the letter `Y` (the “Other” option) **in addition to any other applicable letters**.  
- Do **not** add explanations, comments, or any other text.

**How to Handle Edge Cases**  
1. **Exact wording required** – Only count an algorithm if the excerpt *clearly* names it (e.g., “Ridge Regression”, “Random Forest”). Do not infer a method from vague descriptions such as “a linear model” or “tree‑based methods” unless the specific name appears.  
2. **Multiple mentions** – If more than one algorithm from the list is named, list **all** corresponding letters.  
3. **Missing or ambiguous information** – If the excerpt provides no usable information about the algorithms, output `Z`.  
4. **Other algorithms** – If the excerpt names an algorithm that is **not** in the provided list (e.g., “Deep Neural Network”, “Support Vector Regression”), add `Y` to the answer. Do not attempt to map it to a listed option.  

**Answer Options (letter → algorithm)**  
A: Ridge Regression  
B: LASSO Regression

H: XGBoost

U: Customized model ensembles – user‑defined combinations of multiple base learners (e.g., caretEnsemble)  
Y: Other (any algorithm not listed above)  
Z: None of the listed algorithms are mentioned

# Examples:
Example: "Thirteen machine learning algorithms were selected to provide a 
broad comparison (of a variety of modelling techniques) and to be 
consistent with research that has used similar methods (Webb et al., 
2020). To provide a benchmark against the machine learning models, an 
ordinary least squares regression (OLS) model with all the available 
same predictors (in the same manner as the machine learning models) 
was included. The multiple OLS acts as a comparator with penalised 
regression models by fitting the data without any regularisation, making 
it prone to overfitting compared to models with regularisation. Addi
tionally, two other comparison models akin to those used in similar 
research were fitted (Buckman et al., 2021a). The first was an OLS 
regression model with only the pre-treatment GAD-7 score included, as 
initial symptom severity is one of the most important factors considered 
by clinicians in predicting treatment outcomes (and allocating treat
ment) for their patients (Amati et al., 2018; Buckman et al., 2021b; 
O’Driscoll et al., 2021). Separately, a null model was created using the 
mean post-treatment GAD-7 score in the training and validation dataset 
as the prediction for all test dataset cases. Five models based upon elastic 
net regularised regression (ENR; Friedman et al., 2010) were included 
(alpha parameters were 0.0; full ridge, 0.25, 0.50, 0.75 and 1.0; full 
lasso), using the ridge and least absolute shrinkage and selection oper
ator (lasso) penalisations to reduce overfitting. Two spline regression 
models were used: adaptive splines (Friedman, 1991) and adaptive 
polynomial splines (Stone et al., 1997). In addition, two 
decision-tree-based algorithms were used: random forest (Breiman, 
2001) and Bayesian Additive Regression Trees (BART; Chipman et al., 
2010). Lastly, three support vector machines (SVM) were included with 
linear, polynomial and radial kernels (Dimitriadou et al., 2009). Full 
details of the models are presented in Supplementary Material 1 
(Table 3). 
SuperLearner (van der Laan et al., 2007), the chosen ensemble 
modelling tool, uses cross-validation to estimate the predictive perfor
mance of individual machine learning algorithms, and ultimately de
termines the most accurate model within the ensemble." (Section 2.4; p.2)
