**Task Overview**  
You are an expert research assistant. Your job is to read the supplied excerpt and **assign every applicable code letter** from the list below that correctly describes the *inner‑loop resampling method* used in the study.  

**Instructions – follow these rules exactly**  

1. **Read the excerpt carefully.**  
2. **Select all letters that apply** based strictly on what is *explicitly stated* in the excerpt.  
   - If the excerpt mentions a method that **matches one of the answer options**, choose the corresponding letter.  
   - If the excerpt mentions a correct method **not listed among the options**, choose **Y (Other)** *in addition to any other applicable letters*.  
   - If the excerpt contains **no information** about the inner‑loop resampling method, choose **Z (Not reported)** *and no other letters*.  
3. **Do not infer, guess, or extrapolate** beyond the exact wording of the excerpt.  
   - If the wording is ambiguous or could refer to more than one method, treat it as **not reported** and return **Z**.  
   - If the excerpt states that “the default method was used” without naming it, select **A** (Resampling method not specified, stated that default method was used).  
4. **Output format** – return a **single line** containing a comma‑separated list of the selected letters **without spaces** (e.g., `A,C,E`).  
   - If only one letter applies, output that single letter (e.g., `D`).  
   - If multiple letters apply, list them in **alphabetical order**.  
5. **Do not provide any explanation, rationale, or additional text**—only the list of letters as described.  

**Target Variable**: Type of resampling method (inner loop) – see Section 3.2 “tunevalues”.

**Answer Options**  

- **A** – Resampling method not specified, stated that default method was used  
- **B** – Single k‑fold CV  
- **C** – Repeated k‑fold CV  
- **D** – Leave‑one‑out‑cross‑validation (LOOCV)  
- **E** – Leave‑group‑out‑cross‑validation (LGOCV)  
- **F** – Bootstrap  
- **G** – Optimism‑corrected bootstrap  
- **H** – Rolling window CV  
- **I** – Expanding window CV  
- **J** – Blocked CV  
- **Y** – Other (a correct method not listed)  
- **Z** – Not reported (no information in the excerpt)  

**Excerpt**: {context}

Example: "Through training the ensemble using 10-fold internal cross-validation within the training 
sample (repeated ten times), the BART algorithm produced the lowest 
prediction error when generalising to the validation dataset (MSE [95 % 
CI]=16.72 [16.12; 17.32]; MAE=3.16; R 2 =0.38; see Table 2)." (Section 3.1; p.3)
 True Answer: ['Y']
 Excerpt: {context} Answer:Example: "We used these data for repeated cross-validation. In our cross-validation procedure, we randomly split these 2,258 observations into ten approximately equal parts, and iteratively used nine as training data and the remaining one as validation data until all parts had functioned as validation data once. We repeated this procedure ten times to decrease the influence of chance." (Section "RQ1"; p.4)

Answer:


**Task**  
Extract the numeric value for the target variable **R²** from the supplied excerpt of a research paper.  

**Context**  
You will be given a short passage (the *Excerpt*) taken verbatim from a research article. The passage may contain performance metrics for classification (AUC, ACC, BACC) and regression (R², r).  

**Output Format**  
Return **exactly** a JSON object with two fields:

```json
{
  "value": <number or "Z">,
  "R2_version": "<text>"
}
```

* `value` – the numeric R² reported in the excerpt, **as it appears** (no rounding, no units).  
  *If the excerpt does not contain an R² value, or the value cannot be determined, set this field to the string `"Z"`.*  

* `R2_version` – a brief label of the R² variant the authors mention (e.g., `"adjusted R²"`, `"pseudo‑R²"`).  
  *If the excerpt mentions R² but does not specify a version, set this field to `"not specified"`.  
  If the excerpt contains no R² at all, also set this field to `"not specified"`.*

**Strict Extraction Rules**  

1. **Copy verbatim**: Use the exact numeric token from the excerpt; do not round, truncate, or convert units.  
2. **No inference**: Do not calculate, estimate, or infer R² from other numbers (e.g., from r or from model equations).  
3. **No additional text**: Apart from the JSON object above, output nothing else (no explanations, no comments).  
4. **Missing data**: If the excerpt provides no R² value, return `"value": "Z"` and `"R2_version": "not specified"`.  
5. **Ambiguity**: If multiple R² values appear, select the **highest** one reported for the validation sample (internal or external).  
6. **Version identification**: Look only for explicit qualifiers attached to R² (e.g., “adjusted”, “cross‑validated”, “pseudo”). If a qualifier is present, copy it exactly; otherwise use `"not specified"`.

**Optional Reasoning (do not include in final answer)**  
- Scan the excerpt for any occurrence of “R²”, “R-square”, or “R2”.  
- Record the numeric token that follows the first such occurrence (or the highest if several).  
- Check the surrounding words for version descriptors.  

**Example**  

*Excerpt:* “The model achieved an adjusted R² of 0.78 on the external validation set.”  

*Correct answer:*  

```json
{
  "value": 0.78,
  "R2_version": "adjusted"
}
```

You are an expert research assistant. Your task is to extract information from research paper excerpts according to a predefined coding scheme.
Instructions:
Provide the answer in a few words or a short phrase, taken strictly from the document.

If the requested information is not present in the excerpt, answer with Z.

Target Variable: Specify R² verison
Description: Note the highest performance metrics achieved in the validation sample (either internal or external) for AUC, ACC, and BACC in classification tasks, and for R² and r in regression tasks. Briefly specify which R² version the authors report or write "not specified".; 
Excerpt:
{context}
Answer:


**You are an expert research assistant.** Your task is to extract a single, exact piece of information from a research‑paper excerpt according to the coding scheme below.

---

### Target Variable  
**R² version** – the specific version of the coefficient of determination reported by the authors (e.g., “adjusted R²”, “pseudo‑R²”, “Nagelkerke R²”, “R²”, or “not specified”).

---

### Extraction Instructions  

1. **Read the excerpt** that follows the line `Excerpt:`.  
2. **Locate the exact wording** the authors use to describe the R² metric.  
   - If a version is explicitly named (e.g., “adjusted R²”, “Nagelkerke R²”, “pseudo‑R²”), copy that phrase **verbatim** (preserve case, spacing, and any hyphens).  
   - If the authors only write “R²” with no qualifier, answer with `R²`.  
   - If the authors do **not** mention any R² metric, answer with `Z`.  
3. **Do not** infer, guess, paraphrase, or add any extra words. Use only the exact token sequence that appears in the excerpt.  
4. **If multiple R² versions appear**, select the one presented as the primary result (the one associated with the highest reported performance). If you cannot determine which is primary, answer with `Z`.  
5. **If a numeric R² value is present but no version label**, treat the label as `R²`.  

---

### Output Format  

- Provide **exactly one short phrase** (or the single character `Z`) on the line that begins with `Answer:`.  
- No additional text, explanation, or punctuation (other than what appears in the extracted phrase) is allowed.  

---

### Edge‑Case Handling  

- **Embedded label**: If the label appears within a longer sentence (e.g., “we report the adjusted R² = 0.73”), extract only the label (`adjusted R²`).  
- **Hyphenation/spacing**: Preserve the original hyphenation and spacing exactly as in the source.  
- **Absent label**: Return `Z`.  

---

### Example  

```
Excerpt:
The model achieved an adjusted R² of 0.78 in the validation set.
Answer: adjusted R²
```

**Task Overview**  
You are a research‑assistant tasked with **classifying a single excerpt from a research paper** according to a predefined coding scheme.

---

### 1. Context (provided by the user)  
```
{context}
```

### 2. Target Variable  
**Confidence intervals for performance metrics** – determine whether the excerpt *explicitly* reports confidence intervals (or an equivalent statistical interval) for any performance metric (e.g., accuracy, F1‑score, AUC, etc.).

### 3. Answer Options (choose **exactly one**)  

| Code | Meaning |
|------|---------|
| 1    | No – the excerpt does **not** contain any confidence interval (or equivalent) for a performance metric. |
| 2    | Yes – the excerpt **explicitly** includes a confidence interval (or equivalent) for a performance metric. |
| Z    | Not applicable – the excerpt provides **no information** about confidence intervals **and** it is impossible to determine presence or absence (e.g., the excerpt is unrelated, missing, or entirely ambiguous). |

---

### 4. Decision Rules  

1. **Strict textual evidence only** – select “2” only if the excerpt contains a clear statement such as “95 % CI = [0.78, 0.84]”, “confidence interval”, “credible interval”, “error bars representing …”, or any mathematically equivalent interval explicitly tied to a performance metric.  
2. Select “1” if the excerpt discusses performance metrics **without** any accompanying interval information.  
3. Select “Z” when:  
   - The excerpt does not mention performance metrics at all.  
   - The excerpt is so truncated or vague that you cannot ascertain whether an interval is present or absent.  
   - The excerpt refers to a figure/table without providing the interval values in text.  

4. **No inference or guesswork** – do not infer intervals from p‑values, standard deviations, or narrative descriptions unless the interval is explicitly stated.  

5. **Edge‑case handling** –  
   - If multiple intervals are reported, the presence of any one suffices for “2”.  
   - If the interval is reported for a *different* variable (e.g., a demographic characteristic) and not for a performance metric, treat it as “1”.  

---

### 5. Output Format  

- Return **only** the chosen code (`1`, `2`, or `Z`).  
- **Do not** include any explanation, punctuation, or additional characters (e.g., no “Answer:”, no newline before/after).  

**Example valid outputs:** `1` or `2` or `Z`

---

### 6. Optional Reasoning (for internal verification only)  

If you wish to double‑check your decision, you may briefly note the exact phrase that triggered your choice, but **do not** include this note in the final response.  

---

**Proceed now:** classify the excerpt according to the rules above.

| Letter | Meaning |

7. **Output format:**  
   - Provide **exactly one line** containing the selected letter(s) (or `Z`).  
   - No surrounding text, punctuation other than commas between letters, or whitespace.

 Excerpt: {context} Answer:Example: "In order to explore the importance of each trait in predicting leadership role occupancy on the test data, we examined the increment in the prediction error after perturbing the predictors (Fisher, Rudin, & Dominici, 2019). We perturbed single predictor variables by randomly changing their values 200 times and keeping all other values constant to calculate the “loss drop” in the test set. The loss drop refers to the increase in the quadratic mean of the difference between the predicted likelihood (ranging from 0% to 100%) and the actual leadership role occupancy (0% or 100%).Note that 11.4% of the individuals in the test set were in a leadership role, which means that if a model were to predict 0% (i.e., no leadership role occupancy) for each individual, the root mean squared error (RMSE) would have been 11.4%. Thus, the RMSE will increase when the perturbations result in a decrease (i.e., drop) in predictive performance." (Section "RQ2a"; p.6)

"To further explore the interaction between BFI traits and NFC, we present the RF predictions on the test set against BFI and NFC scores in Fig. 5. In this figure, each person is denoted as either a blue circle or a gray triangle. Blue circles are used when the RF predicts leadership role occupancy (N = 129) and as gray triangles when the RF predicts no leadership role occupancy. As illustrated in Fig. 5, across all interactions, the RF frequently predicted leadership role occupancy for individuals scoring higher (at least 4) on NFC. Further, this higher NFC often resulted in predicted leadership role occupancy for individuals higher on openness (>3.5), conscientiousness (>3.0), and extraversion (>3.0); lower on neuroticism (>3.0); and not too high on agreeableness (<4.5). Note that these findings are largely in line with the accumulated local effects plotted in Fig. 3, in which we visualized the effects of each trait separately." (Section RQ2c"; p.8)

"The importance of interactions between traits in predicting leadership role occupancy is visualized in Fig. 4a. This figure shows that NFC has the strongest interaction effect (H-statistic), meaning that interactions between NFC and the other traits contribute the most to leadership role occupancy predictions. To better understand the interactions between NFC and the other traits, we computed the H-statistic for each pair with NFC. As illustrated in Fig. 4b, the interaction between openness and NFC contributed the most in predicting leadership role occupancy, which we explore next." (Section "RQ2c"; p. 8)

**Task Overview**  
You are a research‑assistant tasked with coding a single excerpt from a research paper. For each excerpt you must assign **exactly one** code from the list below that best reflects the authors’ statements about *generalizability of model performance and variable‑importance measures*.

**Instructions – Follow **strictly** the source excerpt; do not infer, guess, or add any information that is not explicitly present.**

1. **Read the excerpt carefully.**  
2. **Determine which of the three answer options (0, 1, 2) matches the authors’ statements** about generalizability, **or select “Z”** if the excerpt contains no relevant information.  
3. **Return ONLY the selected symbol** (e.g., `1` or `Z`). **No additional text, explanations, or formatting** is allowed.  

**Handling Missing or Ambiguous Information**  
- If the excerpt **does not mention generalizability at all**, output `Z`.  
- If the excerpt contains **multiple statements that belong to different categories**, choose the category that best captures the *overall* stance, prioritizing the most explicit claim.  
- If the excerpt is **unclear or contradictory**, default to the most conservative interpretation (i.e., the lower‑numbered applicable code).  

**Answer Options (choose one)**  

| Code | Meaning (must be explicitly stated in the excerpt) |
|------|----------------------------------------------------|
| **0** | No statements regarding generalizability are present. |
| **1** | Authors claim that their model(s) **generalize beyond the sampled data** (e.g., “our model trained on U.S. participants is applicable to other cultures”). |
| **2** | Authors limit their conclusions **to the analyzed data** or explicitly **question** any generalization beyond the sampling frame (e.g., “results may not hold in other regions”). |
| **Z** | The excerpt provides **no information** about generalizability (i.e., none of the above apply). |

**Optional Reasoning (for internal verification only – do NOT output)**  
- Identify any sentence that directly mentions “generalize”, “extend to”, “apply to other populations”, or similar phrasing.  
- Verify whether the wording is **definitive** (supporting Code 1) or **cautious/limiting** (supporting Code 2).  
- If no such wording exists, assign Code 0 or Z as appropriate.

**Input Format**  

```
Target Variable: Generalizability of model performance and variable importance measures
Excerpt:
{Insert the paper excerpt here}
```

**Output Format**  

```
<single character: 0, 1, 2, or Z>
```

**Example**  

```
Target Variable: Generalizability of model performance and variable importance measures
Excerpt:
“The model was trained on a sample of U.S. adults and is expected to perform similarly in European populations.”

Answer:
1
```
